Caretaker's Duties and Use of Mosque Courtyard
Caretaker's authority over mosque courtyard and endowment income

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the permissible uses of the mosque courtyard by the caretaker and identify the required disposal of any income generated.

Core Idea
Caretaker can allow trade in mosque courtyard only for mosque benefit; income must be given in charity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the limits of a mosque caretaker's authority. It addresses whether the caretaker can build shops or allow trade in the mosque courtyard, and what should be done with any income generated. The ruling highlights the principle that mosque property must be used for the mosque's benefit, not for personal gain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The caretaker considers allowing trade in the mosque courtyard or placing rented benches. Is this permissible?
2
Determine the hukm
The text says 'la ba'sa' (there is no harm) if it is for the improvement (salah) of the mosque. So it is permissible (mubah) under that condition.
But building shops permanently is not allowed.
3
Identify the conditions
The courtyard must not be a public thoroughfare (mamarr al-ammah). Also, the income must not be spent on the caretaker or imam, but given in charity to the poor.
4
Understand the ruling on mixing endowments
The caretaker may mix produce from different mosque endowments regardless of whether the endower is the same or different.
5
Appointment of caretaker without judge
Later Hanafi fatwa allows neighbors to appoint a caretaker without informing the judge, due to judicial corruption.
Earlier opinion differed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قدمنا شيئاً من أحكام المسجد عند قوله ولا نقشه بالجص وماء الذهب من مكروهات الصلاة وفي المجتبى : لا يجوز لقيم المسجد أن يبني حوانيت في حد المسجد أو فنائه، قيم يبيح فناء المسجد ليتجر فيه القوم أو يضع فيه سرراً أجرها ليتجر فيها الناس فلا بأس إذا كان لصلاح المسجد ويعذر المستأجر إن شاء الله تعالى إذا لم يكن ممر العامة وفناء المسجد ما كان عليه ظلة المسجد إذا لم يكن ممر العامة المسلمين ولا يجوز صرف تلك الأجرة إلى نفسه ولا إلى الإمام بل يتصدق به على الفقراء، ولا بأس للقيم أن يخلط غلة أوقاف المسجد المختلفة اتحد الواقف أو اختلف. عن مشايخ بلخ : مسجد له أوقاف ولا قيم فيه فجمع بعض أهل محلته غلاتها وأنفقها في حصره وأدهانه وحشيشه لم يضمن ديانة استحساناً، ولو ثبت عند الحاكم ضمنه وفي تولية أهل المحلة قيماً على أوقافه بدون إذن القاضي اختلاف المشايخ في فتاوى الفضلي، وأفتى مشايخنا أنه يصير متولياً، ثم اتفق المتأخرون وأستأذونا أن الأفضل أن ينصبوا متولياً ولا يعلموا به القاضي في زماننا لطمع القضاة في أموال الأوقاف تنازع أهل المحلة والباني في عمارته أو نصب المؤذن أو الإمام فالأصح أن الباني أولى به إلا أن يريد القوم ما هو أصلح وقيل الباني بالمؤذن أولى وإن كان فاسقاً بخلاف الإمام، والباني أحق بالإمامة والأذان وولده من بعده وعشيرته أولى بذلك من غيرهم. وفي المجرد عن أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه أن المسجد ونصب الإمام والمؤذن إذا تأهل للإمامة اهـ. مصالح بجميع المتقدمون منه ، الباني أولى صلحاً وأقر أهلها عليها هذا ما ظهر لي قوله : (لكن لو قال صلوا فيه جماعة صلاة أو صلاتين يوماً أو شهراً لا يكون مسجداً) قال الشيخ علاء الدين في شرح الملتقى : لعله مفرع على أن التوقيت مبطل
We have already presented some rulings of the mosque regarding his statement 'Do not plaster it with gypsum and gold water' from the disliked acts of prayer. In al-Mujtaba: It is not permissible for the caretaker of the mosque to build shops within the boundary of the mosque or its courtyard. A caretaker may permit the mosque courtyard for people to trade in it, or place benches rented for people to trade, there is no harm if it is for the improvement of the mosque, and the tenant is excused if Allah wills, provided it is not a public thoroughfare. The courtyard of the mosque is what was under the canopy of the mosque if it is not a public thoroughfare of Muslims. It is not permissible to spend that rent on himself nor on the imam, but rather to give charity to the poor. There is no harm for the caretaker to mix the produce of different endowments of the mosque whether the endower is the same or different. From the mashayikh of Balkh: A mosque has endowments and no caretaker, so some residents of its neighborhood collected its produce and spent it on its mats, oils, and grass; he is not liable by din (religiously) as an istihsan, but if established before the judge he would be liable. Regarding the appointment by the neighbors of a caretaker for its endowments without the judge's permission, there is disagreement among the mashayikh in Fatawa al-Fadli. Our mashayikh gave fatwa that he becomes a caretaker. Then the later scholars agreed and sought permission from us that it is better that they appoint a caretaker and not inform the judge in our time due to the greed of judges regarding endowment funds. Dispute between the neighbors and the builder regarding its maintenance or appointment of muezzin or imam: the most correct is that the builder has priority unless the people want what is more beneficial. It is said that the builder has priority regarding the muezzin even if he is sinful, unlike the imam. The builder is more entitled to the imamate and adhan, and his son after him, and his clan are more entitled than others. In al-Mujarrad from Abu Hanifa: The mosque and the appointment of imam and muezzin, if he is qualified for imamate. End. All interests from all the earlier [scholars], the builder is first. And this is what appeared to me. His statement: (But if he says 'pray in it in congregation for one prayer or two prayers, a day or a month', it is not a mosque.) Shaykh Ala al-Din said in Sharh al-Multaqa: Perhaps it is based on that timing invalidates [the waqf].
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage discusses management of mosque property and personnel. The caretaker (qayyim) cannot build shops on mosque premises, but can allow temporary use of the courtyard for trade if it benefits the mosque (e.g., renting space for stalls), provided it does not obstruct a public thoroughfare. The income must be given in charity to the poor, not used for personal or imam's benefit. The caretaker may also merge income from different endowments. If a mosque has no caretaker, local residents may collect and spend the produce without personal liability unless a judge rules otherwise. Appointing a caretaker without the judge's permission is valid according to later Hanafi fatwa, and it is even preferable not to involve the judge due to corruption. When there is a dispute between the mosque's builder and the residents over maintenance or appointment of staff, the builder has priority: he is more entitled to choose the imam and muezzin, and his descendants and clan have precedence. However, if the community chooses someone more qualified, that may override the builder's choice, especially for the imam (as opposed to the muezzin). A final note reiterates that a time-limited permission to pray does not create a permanent mosque.

Key Terms
قيم المسجد (qayyim al-masjid): The person entrusted with managing the mosque's affairs and endowments.
فناء المسجد (finā’ al-masjid): The courtyard or open area attached to the mosque, especially under its canopy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must be done with rent collected from allowing trade in the mosque courtyard?
A) It may be used for the caretaker's salary
B) It must be given in charity to the poor
C) It may be spent on mosque repairs
D) It is given to the imam

Key Takeaway
The mosque caretaker may authorize temporary trade in the courtyard for mosque benefit, but any income must be given in charity, not used for personal or mosque expenses.

Builder's Priority in Mosque Appointments
Builder's rights in mosque staff appointment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school gives the builder priority in appointing the imam and muezzin, and identify when the community can override that choice.

Core Idea
The builder is more entitled to appoint mosque staff, unless the community selects a more qualified candidate.

This passage addresses disputes between the mosque builder and local residents over mosque maintenance and staff appointments. It establishes the builder's precedence, but also allows community preference for a better candidate, especially for the imam. The text also touches on the inheritance of this right by the builder's son and clan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
There is a conflict between the residents (ahl al-mahallah) and the builder (al-bani) over maintenance or appointment of muezzin and imam.
2
State the Hanafi position
The most correct (al-asahh) is that the builder has priority (awla) except if the people want what is more beneficial (aslah).
3
Distinguish between imam and muezzin
It is said that the builder has priority for the muezzin even if he is sinful (fasiq), but for the imam, the community can choose a better candidate.
This suggests different standards for the two roles.
4
Note the inherited right
The builder is most entitled to imamate and adhan, and his son after him, and his clan (ashiratuhu) have priority over others.
This is from al-Mujarrad of Abu Hanifa.
5
Understand the final note
If someone says 'pray in it in congregation for one prayer or two' it does not become a mosque. This underscores that a mosque must be permanently dedicated (waqf).

Source Text

Prohibitions and Permissions for the Mosque Custodian
States prohibitions and permissions for custodian regarding building, leasing, mixing waqf yields

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibitions and permissions for a mosque custodian regarding building, leasing, and mixing waqf yields, and apply the condition of public benefit to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
A mosque custodian cannot use waqf for personal gain; any commercial use must benefit the mosque and not obstruct public thoroughfare.

The mosque custodian (qayyim) is entrusted with managing the mosque's property and endowments. But his authority is not absolute. This passage from al-Mujtaba and the scholars of Balkh clarifies where the lines are drawn, balancing the mosque's needs with public rights and the sanctity of waqf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the custodian's prohibited act
Read the first ruling: building shops in the mosque boundary or courtyard is not permissible. Note the act (building shops) and the ruling (impermissible).
This prohibition applies even if the custodian intends personal gain.
2
Identify the permissible act and its conditions
The custodian may permit the courtyard for trade or place benches for rent, but only if two conditions are met: it is for the benefit of the mosque, and it is not a public thoroughfare.
If the courtyard is a public path, it cannot be used for trade.
3
Determine the disposition of rental income
The rent from such leasing must not go to the custodian or the imam; rather, it must be given in charity to the poor.
This prevents personal enrichment from waqf property.
4
Analyze the rule on mixing waqf yields
The custodian is permitted to mix the produce (yields) of different endowments of the mosque, whether the endower is the same or different.
This facilitates practical management.
5
Apply istihsan in the Balkh case
If there is no custodian and a resident collects waqf yields and spends them on mosque needs (mats, oil, hay), he is not liable in religion by istihsan (juristic preference). However, if a judge rules against him, he becomes liable.
Istihsan overrides strict qiyas due to public benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المجتبى : لا يجوز لقيم المسجد أن يبني حوانيت في حد المسجد أو فنائه، قيم يبيح فناء المسجد ليتجر فيه القوم أو يضع فيه سرراً أجرها ليتجر فيها الناس فلا بأس إذا كان لصلاح المسجد ويعذر المستأجر إن شاء الله تعالى إذا لم يكن ممر العامة وفناء المسجد ما كان عليه ظلة المسجد إذا لم يكن ممر العامة المسلمين ولا يجوز صرف تلك الأجرة إلى نفسه ولا إلى الإمام بل يتصدق به على الفقراء، ولا بأس للقيم أن يخلط غلة أوقاف المسجد المختلفة اتحد الواقف أو اختلف. عن مشايخ بلخ : مسجد له أوقاف ولا قيم فيه فجمع بعض أهل محلته غلاتها وأنفقها في حصره وأدهانه وحشيشه لم يضمن ديانة استحساناً، ولو ثبت عند الحاكم ضمنه
In al-Mujtaba: It is not permissible for the custodian of the mosque to build shops in the boundary of the mosque or its courtyard. If the custodian permits the mosque courtyard for people to trade in, or places benches there to rent for people to trade on, there is no problem if it is for the benefit of the mosque, and the tenant is excused, Allah willing, if it is not a public thoroughfare. The courtyard of the mosque is what was under the shade of the mosque if it is not a public thoroughfare for Muslims. It is not permissible to spend that rent on himself or on the imam; rather he should give it in charity to the poor. There is no problem for the custodian to mix the produce of different endowments of the mosque, whether the endower is the same or different. From the scholars of Balkh: A mosque has endowments and no custodian, so some residents of its neighborhood collected its produce and spent it on its mats, oils, and hay; he is not liable in religion by istihsan. And if it was established before the judge, he would be liable.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage details the duties and limitations of a mosque's custodian (qayyim). The jurists emphasize that the custodian cannot build permanent commercial structures within the mosque's boundaries for personal gain. However, if the courtyard can be used for trade without obstructing the public right of way and for the mosque's benefit, it is permissible. The rent must not be taken by the custodian or imam but given to the poor. The custodian may also mix yields from different endowments if it is practical. A notable case from Balkh shows that if there is no custodian and a resident collects and spends the yield on the mosque's necessities, he is not personally liable (by istihsan) unless a judge rules against him. This highlights the Hanafi principle of istihsan overriding strict qiyas in cases of public benefit.

Key Terms
قيم المسجد (Qayyim al-Masjid): The custodian or supervisor of the mosque and its endowments.
فناء المسجد (Fina' al-Masjid): The courtyard or open area of the mosque, often under the shade of the building.
غلة (Ghullah): The produce or income generated from waqf property (e.g., rent, crops).
استحسان (Istihsan): Juristic preference, a secondary source of law that overrides qiyas when public interest dictates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What should the custodian do with rent obtained from leasing part of the mosque courtyard for trade?
A) Keep it for himself.
B) Give it to the imam.
C) Give it in charity to the poor.
D) Use it to pay for mosque repairs.

Key Takeaway
A mosque custodian has limited authority: commercial use of waqf property requires mosque benefit, no public obstruction, and proceeds must go to the poor.

Rulings on Mosque Property: Surplus and Structural Changes
A compilation of waqf rulings on mosque property use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on using mosque candles, leasing space, and making structural changes to a mosque.

Core Idea
Mosque property is sacred; its use requires donor permission and strict adherence to endowment intent.

This lesson compiles several rulings from al-Quniya on the proper management of mosque endowments. The Hanafi school emphasizes that mosque property, whether movable or fixed, must be used only in the manner intended by the donor or for the mosque's core religious purpose. Unauthorized use, even if customary, is prohibited.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Recognize that all rulings here stem from the sanctity of waqf property. Actions that divert mosque assets from their original purpose are invalid or prohibited.
2
Analyze the Candle Ruling
The imam or mu'azzin cannot take leftover candles without the donor's permission, even if custom permits it. Permission is an explicit condition.
Custom does not override explicit conditions in waqf.
3
Examine Leasing and Commercial Use
Leasing mosque space for commerce (e.g., to a shoemaker) is invalid. The custodian's contract is void.
Mosque property cannot be used for profit-generating activities unrelated to worship.
4
Review Structural Changes
Making windows is disliked; removing walls without clear benefit is impermissible; placing beams on walls is not allowed even from endowment. Building a shop for prayer in the courtyard gives it the ruling of a mosque.
Alterations must serve a manifest religious benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوقف وفي القنية من آخر الوقف : بعث شمعاً في شهر رمضان إلى مسجد فاحترق وبقي منه الموضع الصلاة من غير الأذن : ليس للإمام ولا للمؤذن أن يأخذ بغير إذن الدافع ولو كان العرف في ذلك صریح في ذلك فله ذلك اهـ. وفيها : وكرهوا إحداث الطاقات في المساجد روي ذلك عن ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه. قيم الجامع القديم أجر موضعاً تحت ظلة الباب لبعض الصكاكين لا يصح . لا يجوز إزالة الحائط التي بين المسجدين ليجعلهما واحداً إذا لم يكن فيه مصلحة ظاهرة، وكذا رفع صفته ويضمن القيم ما أنفق فيه من مال المسجد. بنى في فنائه في الرستاق دكاناً لأجل الصلاة يصلون فيه بجماعة كل وقت فله حكم المسجد ولا يوضع الجذع على جدار المسجد وإن كان من أوقافه اهـ.
From the Book of Waqf: And in al-Quniya from the end of waqf: He sent candles in the month of Ramadan to a mosque and they burned, and there remained the place for prayer without permission. He said: It is not for the imam or the mu'azzin to take without the permission of the donor, even if the custom in that is explicit in that; then he may do so. End quote. And in it: They disliked making windows in mosques; that has been narrated from Ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with him. The custodian of the old mosque leased a spot under the canopy of the door to some shoemakers - it is not valid. It is not permissible to remove the wall between two mosques to make them one if there is no apparent benefit in it, and likewise raising its status, and the custodian guarantees what he spent from the mosque's wealth. He built a shop in its courtyard in the rural area for prayer, they pray in it in congregation every time; it has the ruling of a mosque, and the beam should not be placed on the wall of the mosque even if it is from its endowments. End quote.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage from al-Quniya presents several rulings regarding the use of mosque property and structural modifications. First, if candles sent to a mosque for Ramadan burn partially, the imam or mu'azzin may take the remaining amount only with the donor's permission, even if custom permits it. The Hanafi position strictly requires explicit permission. Second, making windows in mosques is disliked, as narrated from Ibn Mas'ud. Third, leasing mosque property (e.g., a spot under the canopy) to a cobbler is invalid. Fourth, removing a wall between two mosques to combine them is not allowed unless there is a clear benefit. Fifth, building a shop in the mosque courtyard for congregational prayer gives it the ruling of a mosque. Sixth, placing a beam on the mosque wall is not allowed even if it is from the mosque's own endowments. These rulings emphasize the sanctity and restricted use of mosque property.

Key Terms
الجذع (al-jidh'): The beam or wooden log placed on the wall of a mosque
الطاقات (al-tāqāt): Windows or openings in the mosque wall

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on taking remaining candles from a mosque fire?
A) Permissible for imam and mu'azzin without permission
B) Permissible only with donor's permission
C) Permissible only with custom
D) Not permissible at all

Key Takeaway
Mosque endowment property must be used strictly according to its original purpose and the donor's conditions.

Conditions in Waqf: Khiyar and Path Stipulation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khiyar in waqf and path stipulation for a mosque.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on khiyar in waqf and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the stipulation of a path for a mosque.

Core Idea
Waqf requires perpetuity; khiyar is void but waqf remains valid; path stipulation is disputed among Hanafi imams.

When a person dedicates part of his house as a mosque, certain conditions like retaining an option (khiyar) or reserving a path can affect the validity of the waqf. The Hanafi school unanimously holds that khiyar is void because waqf must be perpetual, but the mosque itself remains valid. However, the requirement for explicitly stipulating a path is a matter of ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples. This lesson unpacks the proofs and practical consequences of this ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Main Issues
The passage discusses two separate conditions: (1) khiyar (option) attached to the waqf, and (2) the need to stipulate a path for the mosque. First, understand the ruling on each issue and then the basis for the ikhtilaf.
Khiyar is a condition that contradicts perpetuity, while the path is a practical access right.
2
State the Ruling on Khiyar
The text says: 'It is taken as a mosque with the condition of having the option; the mosque is valid and the condition is void.' So the waqf itself is valid, but the condition is null. This is the agreed position in the Hanafi school.
The reason: waqf requires immediate and permanent transfer, so any option to revoke is incompatible.
3
Analyze the Dispute on the Path
Abu Hanifa holds that without expressly stipulating the path, the mosque does not become valid. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the path is automatically included, just as in a lease. Follow the proof each side gives.
The practical difference: which ruling to follow in fatwa.
4
Examine the Sahibayn's Qiyas
They analogize the waqf to a lease: if one leases land without mentioning the path, the lessee still has a right to access. Similarly, the mosque should have an implied right of way.
Abu Hanifa's response: waqf is different because it requires perpetuity (ta'bid), so no analogy to a temporary lease.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the mosque is only valid if the path is stipulated. Under the Sahibayn's view, it is valid even without stipulation. This affects the validity of prayers performed there and the status of the waqf.
The text notes that Qadikhan opposed the Sahibayn, and al-Is'af supported Abu Hanifa, but no final tarjih is given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيها من الوقف : اتخذا مسجداً على أنه بالخيار جاز المسجد والشرط باطل جعل وسط داره مسجداً وأذن للناس في الدخول يعم المسجد من وقد خالف فيه قاضيخان كما مر فتدبر اهـ. ويؤيده قول الإسعاف لأنه لا بد من التأبيد والتوقيت والصلاة فيه إن شرط معه الطريق صار مسجداً في قولهم وإلا فلا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : مسجداً ويصير الطريق من حقه من غير شرط كما لو أجر أرضه ولم يشترط الطريق يصير اهـ.
And in it from the chapter of waqf: They took a mosque with the condition of having the option; the mosque is valid and the condition is void. He made the middle of his house a mosque and permitted people to enter, it becomes a mosque in general. And Qadikhan has contradicted it as has passed, so reflect. End quote. And the statement of al-Is'af supports it because it requires perpetuity and specification. And the prayer in it: if he stipulated the path with it, it becomes a mosque according to their statement; otherwise not according to Abu Hanifa. And the two said: it becomes a mosque and the path becomes part of its right without stipulation, just as if he leased his land and did not stipulate the path, it becomes (the lessee's right) - end quote.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage addresses the conditions for making part of one's house a mosque. If someone declares a part of his house as a mosque but retains an option (khiyar), the mosque is valid but the option is void because waqf requires perpetual and immediate dedication. For a mosque to be valid, access must be assured. Abu Hanifa stipulates that the path must be explicitly reserved for the mosque; otherwise, it does not become a mosque. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the path is automatically included, analogous to a land lease where the path is implied. Qadikhan reportedly differed, but the relied-upon position is the one requiring perpetuity.

Key Terms
الخيار (khiyar): Option or condition of choice in a contract; in waqf, any condition that allows revoking the endowment is void.
التأبيد (ta'bid): Perpetuity, permanence; essential condition for the validity of waqf.
الطريق (tariq): Path or right of way; in the context of making a mosque, the access route for worshippers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone makes the middle of his house a mosque and allows people to enter but does not stipulate a path, what is the ruling?
A) It is a valid mosque
B) It is not a mosque
C) It becomes a mosque only if the path is customary
D) It becomes a mosque but the path is not included

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf, conditions like khiyar are void but do not invalidate the waqf; on the path, Abu Hanifa requires explicit stipulation, while the Sahibayn consider it automatic by analogy to lease.

Rulings on Using Mosque Lamps and Surplus Materials
Rulings on mosque lamp usage, leftover wood, and misappropriated funds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for using a mosque lamp for study, explain the disposal of surplus construction wood, and apply the ruling on misappropriated waqf funds including the istihsan exception.

Core Idea
Mosque property is sacred: lamps, wood, and funds can only be used within strict waqf conditions.

This passage from al-Is'af details specific rulings for mosque mutawallis and users. While seemingly minor, these rules protect the integrity of endowments. You will learn the fine lines between permissible and impermissible use of mosque resources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rulings by Topic
The passage covers three distinct scenarios: mosque lamp usage, surplus construction wood, and misappropriated building funds. Note each ruling separately.
2
Note the Time Limits and Conditions
For the lamp, study is allowed only up to one-third of the night, and all-night lighting requires custom or stipulation. Identify the conditions for permissible use.
3
Understand the Istihsan Exception
For misappropriated funds, the default is prohibition. However, if the mutawalli cannot reach a judge, istihsan permits replacement and discharges responsibility. Recognize that this is a Hanafi juristic preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الإسعاف وليس لمتولي المسجد أن يحمل سراج المسجد إلى بيته، ولا بأس بأن يترك سراج المسجد فيه من المغرب إلى وقت العشاء، ولا يجوز أن يترك فيه كل الليل إلا في موضع جرت العادة فيه بذلك كمسجد بيت المقدس و مسجد النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم والمسجد الحرام، أو شرط الواقف تركه فيه كل الليل كما جرت العادة به في زماننا ويجوز الدرس بسراج المسجد إن كان موضوعاً فيه لا للصلاة بأن فرغ القوم من الصلاة وذهبوا إلى بيوتهم وبقي السراج فيه قالوا : لا بأس بأن يدرس بنوره إلى ثلث الليل لأنهم لو أخروا الصلاة إلى ثلث الليل لا بأس به فلا يبطل حقه بتعجيلهم، وفيما زاد على الثلث ليس لهم تأخيرها فلا يكون لهم حق الدرس. ولو أن قوماً بنوا مسجداً وفضل من خشبهم شيء قالوا : يصرف الفاضل في بنائه ولا يصرف إلى الدهن والحصر. هذا إذا سلموه إلى المتولي ليبني به المسجد وإلا يكون الفاضل لهم يصنعون به ما شاؤوا . ولو جمع مالاً لينفقه في بناء المسجد فأنفق بعضه في حاجته ثم رد بدله في نفقة المسجد لا أن يسعه يفعل ذلك، فإذا فعله وكان يعرف صاحبه ضمن له بدله أو في صرف عوضه في المسجد، وإن كان لا يعرفه رفع الأمر إلى القاضي ليأمره بإنفاق بدله فيه، وإن لم يمكنه الرفع إليه قالوا نرجوا له في الاستحسان الجواز إذا أنفق مثله في المسجد ويخرج عن العهدة فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى .اهـ
And in al-Is'af: It is not for the mutawalli of the mosque to carry the lamp of the mosque to his house. There is no objection to leaving the mosque lamp (lit) from maghrib to isha time. It is not permissible to leave it every night except in places where custom is so, like the Mosque of Jerusalem, the Prophet's Mosque, and the Sacred Mosque, or if the waqif stipulated leaving it every night as is customary in our time. It is permissible to study by the mosque lamp if it is placed (in the mosque) not for prayer, meaning after people have finished prayer and gone to their homes and the lamp remains. They said: There is no objection to studying by its light up to one-third of the night because if they delayed prayer to one-third of the night there is no harm, so his right is not invalidated by their hastening. For what is more than one-third, they cannot delay it, so he does not have the right to study. And if a group built a mosque and some of their wood remained, they said: the surplus should be spent on its construction and not on oil and mats. This is if they handed it over to the mutawalli to build with it; otherwise the surplus is theirs to do with as they wish. And if someone collected money to spend on building a mosque and spent some of it for his own need, then replaced it from another source for the mosque expenditure, it is not permissible for him to do that. If he did it and he knows the owner (of the money?), he guarantees its replacement for the mosque. If he does not know him, he should raise the matter to the judge to order him to spend its replacement in it. If he cannot raise it, they said: we hope by istihsan that it is permissible if he spends an equivalent in the mosque, and he is discharged of responsibility before Allah Almighty. End quote.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings for mosque mutawallis (trustees). First, a mutawalli cannot take the mosque lamp home. Leaving it lit from maghrib to isha is fine, but all night only if customary or stipulated. Study by the lamp is allowed up to one-third of the night. Surplus wood from construction must be used for the mosque unless not entrusted to the mutawalli. Misappropriating mosque funds is not allowed; if someone takes building money for personal use and later replaces it, it is invalid unless he cannot reach the judge, in which case istihsan allows it if he repays the equivalent. These rulings ensure that mosque property is preserved.

Key Terms
المتولي (mutawalli): The trustee or administrator of a mosque endowment
الاستحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference used to permit replacement of misappropriated funds when cannot reach judge

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Until what part of the night is it permissible to study by the light of a mosque lamp?
A) Until midnight
B) Until one-third of the night
C) All night
D) Not permissible at all

Key Takeaway
Mosque property (lamps, wood, funds) must be used strictly according to waqf rules, with limited exceptions based on custom, stipulation, or necessity (istihsan).

Mosque Reconstruction and Entry of Non-Muslims
Rulings on rebuilding and entry permissions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for demolishing a mosque, identify when non-Muslims may enter mosques, and list actions disliked in a mosque.

Core Idea
A mosque may only be rebuilt by locals; non-Muslims may enter for needs; certain actions disrespect the mosque's sanctity.

This lesson covers rulings on the physical mosque: when it can be demolished, who may enter, and how to behave inside. These rules preserve the mosque's dignity and legal inviolability.

Guided Path
1
1. Ruling on rebuilding the mosque
If the builder is not from the neighborhood, they cannot demolish and rebuild; if they are from the neighborhood, it is allowed.
The condition is membership in the local community.
2
2. Ruling on non-Muslim entry
Non-Muslims and dhimmis may enter the Sacred Mosque, al-Aqsa, and other mosques for mosque-related interests or important matters.
The phrase 'لا بأس' indicates permissibility (mubah).
3
3. Ruling on mihrab direction
It is disliked that the mihrab faces a cemetery, wudu area, or bathhouse, unless there is a screen like a wall.
The dislike is makruh tanzih.
4
4. Disliked actions in the mosque
Performing wudu, spitting, blowing the nose, using the mosque as a passage, worldly talk, unsheathing weapons (unless by the blade), and entering without wudu are disliked.
These actions degrade the mosque's sanctity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية : أرادوا نقض المسجد وبناءه أحكم من الأول إن لم يكن الباني من أهل المحلة ليس لهم ذلك، وإن كان من أهل المحلة لهم ذلك اهـ. وفي الحاوي ولا بأس أن يدخل الكافر وأهل الذمة المسجد الحرام وبيت المقدس وسائر المساجد لمصالح المسجد وغيرها من المهمات، ويكره أن يكون محراب المسجد نحو المقبرة أو الميضأة أو الحمام، ويكره التوضؤ في المسجد كالبزق والمخط لما فيه من الاستخفاف، وكذا يكره أن يتخذ طريقاً أو يحدث فيه حديث الدنيا أو يشهر فيه السلاح، فإن شيء منه يستحب أن يأخذ بنصله ويكره الدخول فيه بغير طهارة، وإذا رأى حشيش المسجد فرفعه إنسان جاز إن لم يكن له قيمة فإن كان له أدنى قيمة لا يأخذه إلا بعد الشراء من المتولي أو القاضي أو أهل المسجد أو الإمام، وكذا الجنائز العتق أو الحصر المقطعة والمنابر والقناديل المكسرة والأولى أن تكون حيطان المسجد أبيض غير منقوشة ولا مكتوب عليها، ويكره أن تكون منقوشة بصور أو كتابة اهـ . كان معه : ينافيه. قوله : ( ويكره أن يكون محراب المسجد نحو المقبرة الخ) هذا إذا لم يكن حائل كجدار أما معه
And in al-Bazzaziyya: They wanted to demolish the mosque and rebuild it stronger than the first; if the builder is not from among the people of the neighborhood, they do not have that. If he is from the people of the neighborhood, they have that. End quote. And in al-Hawi: There is no objection to the entry of a disbeliever and dhimmi into the Sacred Mosque, the Mosque of Jerusalem, and all other mosques for the interests of the mosque and other important matters. It is disliked that the mihrab of the mosque be towards a cemetery, the place for wudu, or a bathhouse. It is disliked to perform wudu in the mosque, spitting, and blowing the nose, because it is degrading. Similarly, it is disliked to take it as a path, to speak of worldly matters in it, or to unsheathe weapons in it. If any of that is done, it is recommended to take it by its blade. It is disliked to enter it without ritual purity. If one sees grass of the mosque and lifts it, it is permissible if it has no value; if it has minimal value, he may not take it except after purchasing from the mutawalli, the judge, the people of the mosque, or the imam. Likewise, for freed slaves, torn mats, pulpits, broken lamps - it is better for the walls of the mosque to be white, not decorated or written upon. It is disliked for them to be decorated with images or writing. End quote. And with him: contradicts it. His statement: 'And it is disliked that the mihrab be towards the cemetery etc.' This is if there is no screen like a wall; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage covers a range of rules. Rebuilding a mosque is permissible only if the builder is from the neighborhood. Non-Muslims may enter mosques for legitimate needs. The mihrab direction should not face a cemetery, wudu area, or bathhouse. Spitting and wudu in the mosque are disliked. Carrying weapons is disliked unless gripped by the blade. Entering without wudu is disliked. Grass from the mosque has sanctity; taking it requires purchase if valuable. Walls should be white and free from decoration. The marginal note clarifies that the prohibition of mihrab facing undesirable places applies only if there is no screen.

Key Terms
المحراب (mihrab): The niche in the mosque indicating the direction of prayer

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who may demolish and rebuild a mosque?
A) Any Muslim
B) Only the imam of the mosque
C) A builder from the neighborhood
D) A government official

Key Takeaway
The mosque's sanctity requires that only locals may rebuild it, non-Muslims may enter for needs, and certain actions are disliked to preserve its dignity.

Mosque Property and Adornment
Rulings on taking grass, broken items, and wall decoration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on taking grass from the mosque, identify when purchase is required, and describe the preferred appearance of mosque walls.

Core Idea
Mosque property (grass, broken items) must be bought if valuable; walls should be plain white, free of images or writing.

This lesson deals with the material aspects of the mosque: what may be taken from it and how it should be decorated. These rulings ensure the mosque's property is respected and its focus remains on worship.

Guided Path
1
1. Ruling on taking grass from the mosque
If grass (or similar vegetation) from the mosque has no value, it may be taken. If it has even minimal value, it must be purchased from the mutawalli, judge, or mosque authorities.
The same ruling applies to other mosque property like broken mats, pulpits, and lamps.
2
2. Ruling on wall decoration
It is better (al-awla) for mosque walls to be white without decoration. It is disliked (makruh) to decorate them with images or writing.
This is makruh tanzih according to later Hanafi authorities.

6 min
The Requirement of a Path for a Mosque: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on path condition for mosque validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the necessity of conditioning a public path for a prayer area to become a mosque, and state the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
A prayer area becomes a mosque by dedication alone, but Abu Hanifa requires explicit path conditioning.

When someone designates a space for public prayer, does it automatically become a mosque, or must they also explicitly provide a public path? This short passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a nuanced disagreement within the Hanafi school. Understanding this dispute clarifies the conditions for a valid mosque waqf and the role of implicit necessity in Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the legal issue
Identify the contested question: Does a prayer area become a mosque without explicitly conditioning a public path?
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that without conditioning a path, the area does not become a mosque because the owner retains a right of passage, preventing complete dedication (takhliyah) to Allah.
This reflects a strict view on severance of ownership.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the area becomes a mosque and the path is included by necessity, without explicit condition.
They base this on qiyas (analogy) to land lease.
4
Analyze the qiyas analogy
The Sahibayn compare to leasing land: if someone leases land without stipulating a path, the lessee still has a right of access. Similarly, dedicating a prayer area implies the right of worshippers to access it.
The effective cause (illa) is the necessity of access.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi scholars prefer the Sahibayn's view as it facilitates the establishment of mosques.
This is the operative position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصلاة فيه إن شرط معه الطريق صار مسجداً في قولهم وإلا فلا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : مسجداً ويصير الطريق من حقه من غير شرط كما لو أجر أرضه ولم يشترط الطريق يصير
And prayer in it: if he conditioned a path with it, it becomes a mosque according to their statement, otherwise not according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it becomes a mosque, and the path becomes part of its right without condition, just as if he leased his land and did not stipulate a path, it becomes (included).
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a designated prayer area as a mosque. The issue is whether a dedicated prayer space must have a public path attached to it to become a mosque. Abu Hanifa holds that without conditioning a path, the area does not attain the status of a mosque because the owner retains a right of passage, which prevents complete dedication to Allah. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that the very act of dedicating the space for prayer implies a right of passage, just as leasing land implies a right of access even if not stipulated. The Sahibayn draw an analogy (qiyas) to a land lease: if someone leases land without stipulating a path, the lessee still has a right to use a path by necessity. Similarly, dedicating a place for prayer necessarily includes access for worshippers. Abu Hanifa's position is stricter, requiring explicit severance of all private rights. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to later Hanafi scholars is the view of the Sahibayn, as it facilitates the establishment of mosques.

Key Terms
مسجد (masjid): A place dedicated for prayer with public access, making it a mosque in the full legal sense.
شرط الطريق (shart al-tariq): Explicitly conditioning a public path or entrance when dedicating the prayer area.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why does a prayer area become a mosque without conditioning a path?
A) Because the owner intended it to be a mosque
B) By analogy to leasing land where access is implied
C) Because the Quran commands it
D) Because the area is physically separated

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's operative view is that a dedicated prayer area becomes a mosque by virtue of its purpose, implying a right of access without explicit condition.

Demolishing and Rebuilding a Mosque
Ruling on who may demolish and rebuild a mosque

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding who has the right to demolish and rebuild a mosque and apply it to scenarios involving residency.

Core Idea
Only local residents may demolish and rebuild their mosque; outsiders have no authority.

A mosque is not a private property but a communal waqf. Hanafi law protects the rights of the local community over their mosque's affairs. This lesson examines a concise ruling from al-Bazzaziyya that conditions the authority to demolish and rebuild a mosque on the builder's residency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its nature
The act under discussion is demolishing a mosque and rebuilding it stronger. Consider that this is a significant alteration of a waqf property.
Not routine maintenance, but a major reconstruction.
2
Identify the key factor
The text specifies that the deciding factor is whether the builder is 'min ahl al-mahalla' (from the people of the locality). Determine who qualifies as such.
This includes current residents, not necessarily former or absent ones.
3
Apply the condition to each case
If the builder is a local resident, they have the right to proceed. If not, they do not. State the ruling for each scenario clearly.
No other conditions are mentioned (e.g., need for repair).
4
Understand the rationale
The mosque is for the local community; they have primary say in its management. Outsiders cannot impose changes without local consent.
This aligns with the Hanafi view that the beneficiaries of a waqf have rights over it.
5
Consider implications
Think about how this ruling prevents external interference and preserves local autonomy. Also consider the role of the judge if there is dispute.
The text does not mention judicial oversight, but in practice, disputes may be referred to a qadi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية : أرادوا نقض المسجد وبناءه أحكم من الأول إن لم يكن الباني من أهل المحلة ليس لهم ذلك، وإن كان من أهل المحلة لهم ذلك اهـ
And in al-Bazzaziyya: If they wanted to demolish the mosque and rebuild it stronger than the first, if the builder is not from the people of the locality, they do not have that right; if he is from the people of the locality, they have that right.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This short ruling addresses the authority to demolish and rebuild a mosque in a more solid manner. The key factor is the builder's connection to the locality (mahalla). If the person proposing the project is not a resident of the area, he cannot unilaterally undertake the demolition and reconstruction. However, if he is a local resident, he has the right to do so. This likely stems from the principle that the mosque's users (the local community) have a say in its maintenance. Outsiders may not interfere without permission. The ruling does not specify whether the building must be in need of repair; it assumes the new construction is stronger, implying improvement.

Key Terms
أهل المحلة (ahl al-mahalla): The residents of the neighborhood or locality where the mosque is located.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who has the right to demolish and rebuild a mosque stronger according to al-Bazzaziyya?
A) Any Muslim
B) The waqif only
C) A builder from the people of the locality
D) The judge

Key Takeaway
Authority to demolish and rebuild a mosque belongs to the local residents, not to outsiders.

Dhimmis Entering Mosques: Hanafi Ruling
Ruling from al-Hawi on non-Muslim entry to mosques

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dhimmis entering mosques and identify the conditions under which it is permitted.

Core Idea
Dhimmis may enter mosques for legitimate needs — the mosque is not absolutely restricted to Muslims.

The Hanafi school, based on the text from al-Hawi, permits non-Muslims, including dhimmis, to enter mosques such as the Sacred Mosque and al-Aqsa, provided there is a valid purpose. This lesson examines the ruling and its conditions, highlighting the school's practical approach to coexistence and shared interests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate the action being ruled upon (dhimmi entering a mosque) and the legal assessment (mubah - permissible).
The text uses 'la ba'sa' (no harm), indicating permissibility.
2
Extract the conditions from the text
Find the qualifying phrase that restricts the permission: 'for the interests of the mosque and other important matters'.
The permission is not absolute; it is tied to a legitimate purpose.
3
Apply the ruling to example scenarios
Test the condition against various situations: maintenance, cleaning, emergency repair vs. mere sightseeing.
Only purposes connected to the mosque or significant need justify entry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الحاوي ولا بأس أن يدخل الكافر وأهل الذمة المسجد الحرام وبيت المقدس وسائر المساجد لمصالح المسجد وغيرها من المهمات
And in al-Hawi: There is no harm in a disbeliever and dhimmis entering the Sacred Mosque, the Mosque of Jerusalem, and all other mosques for the interests of the mosque and other important matters.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This ruling from al-Hawi permits non-Muslims (including dhimmis) to enter the Sacred Mosque in Makkah, the Aqsa Mosque in Jerusalem, and all other mosques. The permission is not unconditional; it is tied to legitimate purposes such as matters related to the mosque (like maintenance or cleaning) or other important needs. This shows that mosques are not absolutely restricted to Muslims, especially when there is benefit. The Hanafi school generally allows dhimmis into mosques for valid reasons, unlike some stricter views. The ruling emphasizes practical coexistence and the shared interest in maintaining mosques.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under Islamic rule with a pact of protection (dhimma).
المسجد الحرام (al-masjid al-haram): The Sacred Mosque in Makkah.
بيت المقدس (bayt al-muqaddas): The Mosque of Jerusalem (al-Aqsa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Hawi, why may dhimmis enter mosques?
A) Only for emergency
B) For the interests of the mosque and other important matters
C) Only during construction
D) They are not allowed at all

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits dhimmi entry to mosques for legitimate purposes, emphasizing utility and coexistence.
